The Motive for Composing a Work on Usul al-Din
The author explains why he wrote his poem on usul al-din: to enable convincing others with proofs and refute doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's motivation for composing his poem on usul al-din and the key concept of clarification (al-tabyīn) as moving from uncertainty to clarity.

Core Idea
Usul al-din equips you to prove the truth and dismantle doubts.

Every science has a purpose. The author of Jawharat al-Tawḥīd composed his poem not just to summarize beliefs, but to provide a tool for convincing others and responding to doubts. This lesson explores that motivation and the central idea of clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's words and the commentator's explanation guide your initial understanding.
The passage includes both the main text and a short commentary.
2
Identify the motive
Find where the author states his reason for writing the poem. Notice the key phrases: 'presenting proofs' and 'repelling doubts'. Reflect on why these are the stated goals.
The motive is explicitly given at the start.
3
Understand clarification
The passage then explains that usul al-din needs 'clarification' (al-tabyīn) – defined as extracting something from the realm of uncertainty to the realm of manifestation. Consider how this definition relates to the overall purpose.
The commentator clarifies that 'realm' (al-ḥayyiz) is used metaphorically for a state or condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحامل للمصنف على جعل منظومته في علم أصول الدين على الغير، وإلزامها إياه ، بإيراد الحجج ، ودفع الشبه (١).
ثم بيّن السبب الحامل له على وضع هذه المنظومة في أصول الدين دون غيره من العلوم الواجبة ؛ بقوله : ( يَحْتَاجُ ) أي : الفن المُلقَّبُ بأصول الدين ( لِلتَّبْيين) أي : التَّوضيح ؛ بتصوير مسائله وإثباتها بقواطع الأدلة والبيان: إخراج الشَّيء (٢) مِن حَيّز الإشكال إلى حيز التجلّي(٣).
(١) قوله : ( بإيراد الحجج ) المتبادر من الباء في قوله : (بإيراد) الاستعانة دون السببية ، وإن سُلَّم، وجب حملها على السببية العادية دون الحقيقية ، بدليل ذكر السببية السابقة ، والمراد بالحجج كذلك والشبه : ما هو في زعم الناظر ، لا باعتبار ما في نفس الأمر ، وإلا خرج كلام المخطئ من الحد ، والمقصود دخوله كما تقدم الشنواني.
(٢) عرَّف السيد الشريف الجرجاني البيان بتعريفين ؛ أحدهما ما ذكره الشارح هنا، قال: البيان: إظهار المعنى وإيضاح ما كان مستورًا قبله ، وقيل : هو الإخراج من حيز الإشكال، والفرق بين التأويل والبيان أن التأويل ما يذكر في كلام لا يفهم منه معنى محصل في أول وهلة، والبيان ما يذكر فيما يفهم ذلك لنوع خفاء بالنسبة إلى البعض . اهـ . محي.
(٣) قوله: (من حيز الإشكال ) الحيز في الأصل اسم للمكان ، ثم إنه تجوز فيه هنا عن الصفة ، ثم إن جعلت الإضافة بيانية أي حيز هو الإشكال، وحيز هو التجلي. قال ابن قاسم: فإن قلت : الحيز في ذلك التعريف مجاز وهو ممتنع فيها. ويجاب: بأن التجوز في الحد لا مطلقا، بل يجوز عند وضوح المعنى مع فهم المراد ، ولعل استحالة ثبوت الحيز للمعاني كالإشكال والإيضاح قرينة على المقصود. اهـ. والحاصل: أن الحيّز إنما يكون للأمور المحسوسة ، فشبه الإشكال بجرم ذي حيّز على طريق الاستعارة بالكناية ، وإثباتُ الحيّز تخييل ، أو أنه مستعار للصفة استعارة تصريحية ، والإضافة للبيان ، وكذا يقال فيما بعده ، ولك أن تجعله من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه بجامع الاشتمال.
The motive for the author to make his poem in the science of the foundations of religion for others, and to impose it upon him, by presenting proofs and repelling doubts (1). Then he explained the motive for him to place this poem in the foundations of religion rather than other obligatory sciences, by his saying: 'needs' i.e., the art called the foundations of religion, 'for clarification', i.e., explanation, by depicting its issues and establishing them with definitive proofs and elucidation: the extraction of a thing (2) from the realm of uncertainty to the realm of manifestation (3). (1) His saying: 'by presenting proofs' – the apparent meaning from the letter 'ba' in his saying 'by presenting' is that of seeking assistance, not causality; and if it is conceded, it must be interpreted as ordinary causality, not true causality, as indicated by the mention of the prior causality. And by 'proofs' and 'doubts' is meant what is in the opinion of the observer, not according to what is in reality; otherwise, the speech of the mistaken would exit the definition, and the intention is to include it, as previously mentioned. al-Shanawānī. (2) Al-Sayyid al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī defined 'clarification' (al-bayān) with two definitions; one of them is what the commentator mentions here: he said: 'clarification' is the manifestation of meaning and the explanation of what was previously concealed, and it is also said: it is the extraction from the realm of uncertainty. The difference between interpretation (al-taʾwīl) and clarification is that interpretation is mentioned in speech from which no concrete meaning is understood at first glance, while clarification is mentioned in what is understood for a kind of hiddenness relative to some. End. Muḥyī. (3) His saying: 'from the realm of uncertainty' – 'realm' (al-ḥayyiz) originally means a place, then it is used metaphorically here for the quality. Then if the genitive construction is considered explanatory, i.e., a realm that is uncertainty, and a realm that is manifestation. Ibn Qāsim said: If you say: the realm in that definition is metaphorical and it is impossible in it. The answer: metaphorical usage in definitions is not absolutely prohibited; rather it is permissible when the meaning is clear with understanding of the intent, and perhaps the impossibility of establishing a realm for meanings like uncertainty and clarification is a clue to the intended meaning. End. The conclusion: the realm is only for sensory things, so uncertainty is compared to a body having a realm by way of metaphor by allusion, and asserting a realm is imaginative; or it is borrowed for the quality by explicit metaphor, and the genitive is for explanation, and similarly for what follows. You may also consider it as likening the thing compared to the thing compared with it due to the common ground of inclusion.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage gives the reason why the author composed his poem on usul al-din rather than other obligatory sciences. It states that the motive is to enable one to present proofs and repel doubts, i.e., to convince others and defend the faith. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'by presenting proofs' means using proofs as a means, not a cause; and the proofs and doubts are considered according to the observer's belief, not absolute truth, so that even erroneous arguments are included. Then the passage explains the need for 'clarification' (al-tabyīn), defined as extracting something from the realm of uncertainty to the realm of manifestation. The commentator discusses the term 'realm' (al-ḥayyiz) in the definition, noting it is originally a place but used metaphorically for a state. He addresses the objection that metaphor is inappropriate in definitions, arguing it is allowed when the meaning is clear. He concludes that 'realm' is used metaphorically to compare uncertainty and clarity to physical spaces.

Key Terms
الحامل
إيراد الحجج
دفع الشبه
التبيين
حيز الإشكال
حيز التجلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the purpose of kalam is only to prove one's own beliefs, not to answer doubts or engage opponents.
Correction: The author explicitly includes 'repelling doubts' as a motive, showing that addressing objections and engaging with others is central to the discipline.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the motive for composing the poem on usul al-din?
A) To list all obligatory sciences
B) To present proofs and repel doubts
C) To define the term 'realm'
D) To clarify the difference between interpretation and clarification

Key Takeaway
The author composed his poem on usul al-din to provide a means of presenting proofs and countering doubts, and he defines the core task of the discipline as moving the mind from uncertainty to clear manifestation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this lesson, did you view usul al-din as a tool for argumentation or as a means to clarify your own understanding? How does the author's dual motive (presenting proofs and repelling doubts) challenge you to adopt a more active role in learning?
?
The author defines clarification as moving from the 'realm of uncertainty' to the 'realm of manifestation.' Where in your own faith or knowledge are you still dwelling in uncertainty, and what concrete step will you take today to begin that extraction?
?
If the only reason you studied theology was to convince others, would that be enough? The author also sought to repel doubts—both others' and perhaps his own. When you learn a new belief, do you hold it with clarity or do you let unaddressed doubts linger? What will you do with a doubt you have not yet resolved?

The Best Method: Brevity in Kalam Authorship
States the best method for writing in kalam, emphasizing necessary brevity and including a historical anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of balanced brevity (ikhtisar) in kalam authorship and how it avoids the extremes of prolixity and defective conciseness.

Core Idea
Effective kalam writing requires brevity that neither tires nor confuses.

This lesson introduces the optimal method for composing works in the science of kalam, as derived from the author's poetic statement and its commentary. It emphasizes that while the science needs explanation, one must avoid excessive length that discourages learners, as well as overly concise writing that obscures meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line and its gloss
Start by reading the opening verse and the commentator's explanation of key terms like 'kallat al-himam'. Notice how the principle of brevity is introduced through poetry.
The gloss on 'kallat' meaning 'became tired' sets the tone.
2
Understand the harm of lengthiness
The passage explains that excessive length (tatwil) leads to boredom and prevents weak learners from engaging. Reflect on why this is a problem for theological education.
3
Examine the anecdote of al-Ash'ari
Read the story about Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his teacher al-Jubba'i. Consider how the failed dialectical exchange illustrates the ineffectiveness of overelaborate reasoning.
This story became proverbial and marked al-Ash'ari's turn to Sunni theology.
4
Distinguish between blameworthy extremes
The passage concludes by contrasting blameworthy prolixity (itnab mumill) and defective conciseness (ijaz mukhkill). Understand why only balanced brevity (ikhtisar) is recommended.
The principle: what is necessary to complete an obligation becomes obligatory.

بيانُ الطَّريق الأفضل للتأليف في هذا العلم

لكنْ مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ كَلَّتِ الِهمَمْ فَصَارَ فِيْهِ الِاخْتِصَارُ مُلْتَزَمْ

ثمَّ استدرك على ما يقتضيه احتياجُ هذا الفنّ للتبيين من مزيد التطويل

(لَكِنْ) وإن احتاجَ للتّبيين لا تَنْبغي المُبالغة معه في تطويل العبارة ؛ لأنه مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ المُؤَدِّي إلى الملل والسَّآمة (كَلَّتِ) أي: تعبت (الِهمَمُ)

فهلًا أمتني صغيرًا فأكونَ كأخي الذي مات صغيرًا فلم يُتَب ولم يُعاقب ؟!

فلم يقدِر الجبَّائيُّ على التّخلص من هذا الاعتراض ، وقال لأبي الحسن: أَبِكَ جُنون ؟ فقال : لا ، ولكن وقف حمار الشيخ في العقبة ، فصارت هذه الجملة مَثَلًا ، ومن ذلك الحين ترك أبو الحسن الأشعري دروس الجُبَّائي ، وأخذ ينقض مذهب شيخه ، وينافح عن آراء أهل السُّنَّة والجماعة ، رضي الله تعالى عنه وأرضاه.

فصار فيه الاختصار ملتزم تقريباً على المتعلمين القاصرين ; فظهر من كلام المصنف رحمه الله تعالى منطوقا ومفهوما .. أنَّ الإطناب الممِلَّ مذموم ; لأنَّه يمنع الهمم القاصرة من تعاطيه، والإيجاز المُخلَّ بأداء المقصود كذلك ; لأنَّه لا يوصِلُ إلى صحة فهمه، فيتعيَّن الاختصارُ ; لأنَّ ما لا يتمُّ الواجب إلا به فهو واجب
Statement of the best method for authorship in this science: 'But from lengthiness, the aspirations become weary, so brevity in it became necessary.' Then he [the author] objects to what the need of this art for clarification necessitates of excessive lengthiness: '(But) even if it needs clarification, one should not exaggerate with it in prolonging expression; because it is from lengthiness that leads to boredom and weariness. (Kallat) i.e., became tired (al-himam).' 'Why did you not let me die young so that I would be like my brother who died young and was not judged or punished?!' So al-Jubba'i was unable to extricate himself from this objection, and he said to Abu al-Hasan: 'Are you mad?' He said: 'No, but the shaykh's donkey stopped at the hill.' So this phrase became a proverb, and from that time Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari left the lessons of al-Jubba'i and began to refute the doctrine of his shaykh and defend the views of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, may Allah be pleased with him and grant him contentment. 'So brevity in it became necessary approximately for the weak learners; for it became clear from the words of the author, may Allah have mercy on him, in wording and implication, that causing-boredom prolixity is blameworthy because it prevents weak aspirations from engaging with it, and likewise defective conciseness in conveying the intended meaning because it does not lead to correct understanding. Therefore brevity is determined, because what the obligatory cannot be completed except by it is obligatory.'
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the best method for composing works in the science of kalam. It begins by quoting a line of poetry from the author: 'But from lengthiness, the aspirations become weary, so brevity in it became necessary.' This line states the core principle: excessive length tires the reader, so brevity is required. The commentator then explains that although this science needs clarification, one should not exaggerate in lengthening the expression to the point of boredom. The word 'kallat' is glossed as 'became tired'.

The commentator then relates an anecdote about Imam al-Ash'ari and his teacher al-Jubba'i. The story is presented as an illustration of the failure of excessive dialectical reasoning. A son asks his father (al-Jubba'i) a question about predestination, and the father is unable to answer. When challenged, al-Ash'ari made a remark that became proverbial, and subsequently left his teacher's lessons and adopted Sunni theology. This anecdote serves to show that the method of the Mu'tazilites, with its overelaborate reasoning, was ineffective and led to confusion, whereas the Sunni method is sound.

The passage then returns to the main argument: brevity is necessary for weak learners. It distinguishes between blameworthy prolixity (al-itnab al-mumill) and defective conciseness (al-ijaz al-mukhkill). Both are criticized: prolixity because it prevents the faint-hearted from engaging, and defective conciseness because it fails to convey the meaning. The only correct method is al-ikhtisar (brevity that is not defective), because what is necessary to complete an obligation becomes obligatory itself.

Correction: The anecdote is an illustration of the failure of invalid reasoning and reinforces the need for brevity; the main topic is the principle of balanced brevity in kalam authorship.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The best method for kalam authorship is balanced brevity that makes the content accessible without oversimplification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where in your faith journey are you settling for defective conciseness or exhausting prolixity rather than seeking the balanced truth this lesson advocates?

Stylistic Choices: Tatwil, Ijaz, and Ikhtisar
Defines three rhetorical terms and explains the author's stylistic choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of tatwil, ijaz, and ikhtisar as used by the author, and why the author chose ikhtisar as the preferred style.

Core Idea
Good brevity preserves meaning; excessive length or defective conciseness hinder understanding.

Before diving into the theological content of 'Jawharat al-Tawhid', the author and commentator clarify the stylistic approach. They define three key terms that describe how a text can be written: too long, too short, or just right. This lesson examines each term and explains why the author chose the middle path of balanced brevity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice that the passage begins by naming the poem and then immediately defines terms.
The commentary expands on the definitions, so reading carefully is important.
2
Identify the three main terms
Find the three terms: tatwil, ijaz, and ikhtisar. Also note the sub-definitions of hashw and itnab under tatwil.
These terms are central to understanding the author's methodology.
3
Understand the criticism of tatwil and defective ijaz
Notice that the author explicitly criticizes tatwil (it weakens aspirations) and defective ijaz (it fails to clarify). Reflect on why these are problematic for a teaching text.
This shows the author's awareness of his audience.
4
Define ikhtisar in your own words
Based on the passage, write a one-sentence definition of ikhtisar. Compare it with the commentator's suggestion that it means focusing on core issues and using easy language.
Ikhtisar is the preferred method.

وصف المنظومة بما يُرغَبُ الطالب فيها

وَهَذِهِ أُرْجُوزَةٌ لَقَبْتُها جَوْهَرَةَ التَّوْحِيْدِ قَدْ هَذَّبتها

ههنا ألفاظ ذكرها المصنّف والشَّارحُ رحمهما الله تعالى يجب معرفة حقائقها، وهذه الألفاظ هي: التطويل، والإيجاز، والاختصار، وأما التطويل فهو: أداء المقصود بلفظ زائد على المتعارف لأوساط الناس الذين ليس لهم فصاحة ولا بلاغة ، وينبغي أن يراد به ههنا ما يَشمل الذي سماه أهل البلاغة حشوا وإطناباً ، فأمَّا الحشو :فهو ما تعيَّنت زيادته على أداء أصل المقصود ولم تكن له فائدةٌ وأمَّا الإطناب فهو : الزِّيادة عن أصل المطلوب لفائدة . وأما الإيجاز فهو: أداء المقصود بلفظ أقلّ مِن المُتعارف ، وقد ذم المصنف التطويل صراحة بأن جعل الهمم تَضْعُفُ عن التحصيل بسببه ، وذم الإيجاز المُخلَّ بالمفهوم ; لأنَّ الإيجاز المُخلَّ بالمقصودِ لا تبيين معه ، وقد ذكرَ أنَّ هذا العلم يحتاجُ للتّبيين ، فلم يبق إلا الاختصار ، وهو : ههنا بمعونة المقام - عبارة عن أداء المعنى المقصود بعبارة قليلة من غير إخلال بشيء من المطلوب أداؤُه ، ويشبه أن يكون قد أراد الاقتصار على أمهات المسائل، وترك التفريعات الكثيرة ، وأداء ذلك بعبارة قصيرة سهلة الفهم.
Description of the poem in a way that attracts the student: 'And this is an urjuzah which I have titled Jawharat al-Tawhid, which I have refined.' Here are terms mentioned by the author and the commentator, may Allah have mercy on them both; it is necessary to know their realities. These terms are: tatwil, ijaz, and ikhtisar. As for tatwil (excessive length), it is: delivering the intended meaning with wording that exceeds the conventional norm for average people who have neither eloquence nor rhetoric. And it should be intended here to include what the people of rhetoric call hashw and itnab. As for hashw, it is: that whose addition over the original intended meaning is determined, but it has no benefit. As for itnab, it is: the increase beyond the original desired meaning for a benefit. As for ijaz (conciseness), it is: delivering the intended meaning with wording less than the conventional norm. The author explicitly criticized tatwil by making the aspirations weaken from accomplishment because of it, and he criticized defective ijaz that impairs understanding, because defective ijaz that impairs the intended meaning contains no clarification. And he mentioned that this science needs clarification. So nothing remained except ikhtisar (brevity). And it is here, with the help of context: an expression of delivering the intended meaning with few words without any deficiency in what is required to be conveyed. And it resembles that he intended to confine himself to the core issues, leaving aside the many branches, and conveying that with short, easy-to-understand expression.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage introduces the poem by giving its title: 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' (The Jewel of Monotheism), and states that the author has refined it. The commentator then provides an extended definition of three key technical terms used in the discussion of authorship style: tatwil, ijaz, and ikhtisar.

First, tatwil (excessive length) is defined as using more words than the average person needs, beyond what is conventional. It includes both hashw (useless padding) and itnab (beneficial expansion). Hashw adds nothing beneficial, while itnab adds extra words for a purpose.

Second, ijaz (conciseness) is using fewer words than the norm. The commentator notes that the author criticized both tatwil and defective ijaz: tatwil weakens the reader's aspiration, and defective ijaz fails to clarify the intended meaning, which is unacceptable because this science requires clear explanation.

Third, ikhtisar (balancd brevity) is presented as the middle path: using few words without omitting any necessary meaning. The commentator suggests that in this context, ikhtisar means focusing on the core issues (ummahat al-masa'il) and leaving aside numerous branches, while expressing the essentials in short, easy-to-understand language. The passage underscores that the author's choice of brevity is deliberate and necessary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Brevity simply means using few words, regardless of clarity.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between defective ijaz (which lacks clarity) and proper ikhtisar (which is brief yet clear and complete).

Checkpoint
What is the difference between hashw and itnab according to this passage?
A) Hashw is beneficial, itnab is not.
B) Hashw has no benefit; itnab adds extra words for a benefit.
C) Both are the same.
D) Hashw is longer than itnab.

Key Takeaway
The author chose ikhtisar—a balanced brevity that is neither excessively long nor defectively short—to ensure clarity and preserve meaning in teaching theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

First Objection: Summary vs. Detailed
First objection and three answers regarding 'هذه أرجوزة'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first grammatical objection to the phrase 'هذه أرجوزة' and the three distinct responses offered by the commentator and alternative scholars.

Core Idea
The apparent mismatch between 'this' (summary) and 'urjuzah' (detailed) can be resolved in multiple ways.

When the commentator says 'وهذه أرجوزة', a careful reader notices that 'this' points to summary ideas while 'urjuzah' implies a detailed poem. This creates a grammatical tension. In this lesson, we explore the first objection and how three different answers—including a creative ellipsis—restore harmony.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first objection: 'هذه' refers to summary words in the mind, but 'أرجوزة' denotes something detailed. Identify the mismatch.
The objection hinges on the terms 'mujmal' and 'mufassal'.
2
Examine the commentator's answer
The commentator posits an omitted noun: the underlying sentence is 'مفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة' (the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah). Note that this answer assumes two premises.
This answer is based on admitting that the words in the mind are only summary and that 'urjuzah' is detailed.
3
Discover two alternative answers
Two other answers avoid the ellipsis: first, that the mind can contain detailed organized material; second, that 'urjuzah' may be a name for the summary form itself.
These answers do not require assuming an omitted noun.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each answer resolves the mismatch. Which do you find most convincing? Why?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) مُفَصَّلُ نوع (۱) (هَذِهِ) الألفاظ المُخيلة الدَّالَّة على المعاني المقصودة (1) أشار الشارح الله عليه بقوله: (ومفصل نوع هذه الألفاظ إلى دفع اعتراضين يردان على عبارة المصنف التي هي قوله: (وهذه أرجوزة (لقبتها وحاصل الاعتراض الأوّل أنَّ قولَه : (هذه) اسم إشارة أشار به إلى الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن، والألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مُجملةٌ ، والأرجوزة : اسم للمفصَّل بابا فبابا ، فلم يحصل التطابق بين المبتدأ وخبره ، وقد عُلم أنَّ التطابق بينهما واجب. ومحصَّلُ ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب : أنَّ الكلام ليس على ظاهره، بل هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام على هذا ومفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) فحذف المضاف، وأقام المضاف إليه مقامه، ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ هذا الجواب مبني على تسليم أمرين: الأول: أنَّ الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن التي أشار إليها بـ(هذه) لا تكون إلا مجملة. أ والثاني: أنَّ لفظ (أرجوزة) اسم للمفصل بابا فبابا . ولك أن تجيب على هذا الاعتراض بجوابين آخرين غير جواب الشارح الذي أشارت إليه أنه قد يقوم كلمة (مفصل) ومحصل الجواب الأول منهما : أنا نمنع أنه لا يقوم بالذهن إلا المجمل، وتدعي بالذَّهن المفصَّل المنظَّم المرتب المتّسق ، وهذا هو مذهب أهل التحقيق. ومحصل الجواب الثاني منهما : أنا لا نسلّم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم للمفصل المرتب، بل يجوز الأقرب ؛ لأنه يبعد ملاحظتها عند الوضع بيتا أن تكون اسماً للهيئة المجملة ، بل هذا هو بيتا، وإذا أجبنا بأحد هذين الجوابين لم نكن في حاجة إلى تقدير مضاف . ومحصل الاعتراض الثاني: أنَّ المشار إليه بقوله (هذه) هو ما في ذهن المصنّف وحده؛ فهو جزئي ، والأرجوزة : اسم للألفاظ لا بقيد كونها في ذهن المصنف، بل أعمُّ مِن أنْ تكون في ذهنه أو في ذهن غيره ؛ فهو كلّي ، فلم يتطابق المبتدأ والخبر أيضاً. ومحصل ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب على هذا الاعتراض: أن الكلام ليس على ظاهره أيضًا، وإنَّما هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام (مفصل نوع هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) ، وأنت جد خبير أن هذا الجواب يتضمن تسليم ما ذكره المعترض في اعتراضه، ومنه أن الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره ، وهو مبني على أنَّ أسماء الكتب ونحوها من قبيل علم الجنس أو اسم الجنس، ولك أن تجيب بجواب آخر على هذا الاعتراض ، =
And the detailed kind of these words are the imaginative [words] indicating the intended meanings. (1) The commentator, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated by his saying: (And the detailed kind of these words [is] to repulse two objections that occur regarding the author's statement which is his saying: ('And this is an urjuzah (I have titled it...') The summary of the first objection is that his saying 'this' is a demonstrative pronoun referring to the words present in the mind, and the words present in the mind are summary (mujmal), while 'urjuzah' is a name for the detailed (mufassal) chapter by chapter, so there is no agreement between the subject and predicate, and it is known that agreement between them is obligatory. The summary of what the commentator indicated as an answer: that the speech is not according to its apparent meaning, but rather it is on the assumption of an omitted noun before the subject, and the original speech according to this is 'And the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah', so the noun was omitted and the genitive was put in its place, and it is not hidden from you that this answer is based on admitting two matters: first: that the words present in the mind referred to by 'these' are only summary; and second: that the word 'urjuzah' is a name for the detailed chapter by chapter. And you can answer this objection with two other answers besides the commentator's answer which he indicated: that the word 'detailed' (mufassal) may be [implicit]. The summary of the first of them: we deny that only the summary is present in the mind, and we claim that the detailed, organized, arranged, consistent [material] is present in the mind, and this is the doctrine of the people of verification. And the summary of the second of them: we do not concede that 'urjuzah' is a name for the detailed organized [poem], but rather the closer [meaning] is possible; because it is far-fetched to regard it when coined as a name for the summary form, but rather this is [the case]. And if we answer with either of these two answers, we have no need to assume an omitted noun. And the summary of the second objection: that the referent of his saying 'this' is only what is in the author's mind, so it is particular (juz'i), while 'urjuzah' is a name for the words without restriction of being in the author's mind, but more general than being in his mind or in someone else's, so it is universal (kulli), so the subject and predicate also do not agree. And the summary of what the commentator indicated as an answer to this objection: that the speech is not according to its apparent meaning either, but rather it is on the assumption of an omitted noun before the subject, and the original speech is 'the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah'. And you are fully aware that this answer involves conceding what the objector mentioned in his objection, including that 'urjuzah' is a name for what is in the author's mind and others, and it is based on that names of books and the like are of the type of generic proper noun (ism al-jins) or common noun (ism al-jins). And you can answer with another answer to this objection, =
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a detailed response to two grammatical/logical objections concerning the phrase 'وهذه أرجوزة' (And this is an urjuzah). The first objection argues that 'هذه' (this) refers to the summary words in the mind, while 'أرجوزة' denotes a detailed arrangement, causing a mismatch. The commentator's initial answer involves positing an omitted noun, making the underlying sentence 'مفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة' (the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah). Two alternative answers are then presented: first, that the mind can contain detailed, organized material, not just summary; second, that 'أرجوزة' in its coinage may refer to a summary form, removing the need for an omitted noun. The second objection argues that 'هذه' refers only to the author's mind (particular), while 'أرجوزة' is general (universal). The commentator again resorts to an omitted noun, but this answer concedes the objector's point. Another answer is suggested (though not fully given). The passage demonstrates careful grammatical reasoning typical of kalam commentary.

Correction: The commentator shows that the phrase can be understood with an ellipsis, or by reinterpreting the nature of the mental words or the meaning of 'urjuzah'.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the basis of his answer to the first objection?
A) The mind can hold detailed material.

D) The demonstrative pronoun is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to 'هذه أرجوزة' is resolved either by positing an omitted noun or by reexamining what the mind can contain or what 'urjuzah' means.

?
When you encounter a difficult or seemingly contradictory statement in a religious text, do you immediately assume the problem is with your understanding, or do you consider that there may be multiple valid interpretations? Which of the three answers in this passage most resembles your own approach, and why?
?
The people of verification claim that the mind can hold detailed, organized knowledge. Do you truly believe your mind is capable of grasping detailed theological truths? What specific steps are you taking to fill your mind with such organized knowledge, or are you relying on summaries and shortcuts?
?
The commentator's answer required accepting two premises without question. Are you willing to examine your own assumptions when interpreting sacred texts? What assumptions might you be holding right now that could be preventing you from reaching a deeper understanding of a difficult issue?

Second Objection: Particular vs. Universal
Second objection and its answer concerning particular vs. universal agreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second grammatical objection concerning the particularity of 'هذه' versus the universality of 'أرجوزة', and how the commentator's answer concedes the objection while hinting at an alternative.

Core Idea
The demonstrative 'this' is particular to the author's mind, but 'urjuzah' is a universal term—another apparent mismatch.

The second objection takes a different angle: 'هذه' refers only to what is in the author's mind (particular), while 'أرجوزة' names any such poem, whether in the author's mind or another's (universal). This creates another mismatch. The commentator again resorts to an omitted noun, but in doing so he concedes the objector's premise. We examine this nuanced exchange.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
Read the objection: 'هذه' refers only to the author's mental words (particular), while 'أرجوزة' is general (universal), so they do not agree.
The key terms are 'juz'i' (particular) and 'kulli' (universal).
2
Examine the commentator's answer
The commentator again says the sentence has an omitted noun: the underlying is 'مفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة'. Note that this answer concedes that 'urjuzah' is universal.
This answer is based on treating book names as generic or common nouns.
3
Consider the hinted alternative
The text says you can answer with another answer, but it is not elaborated. Reflect on what other response might avoid conceding the universality of 'urjuzah'.
Think about how redefining the terms might work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) مُفَصَّلُ نوع (۱) (هَذِهِ) الألفاظ المُخيلة الدَّالَّة على المعاني المقصودة (1) أشار الشارح الله عليه بقوله: (ومفصل نوع هذه الألفاظ إلى دفع اعتراضين يردان على عبارة المصنف التي هي قوله: (وهذه أرجوزة (لقبتها وحاصل الاعتراض الأوّل أنَّ قولَه : (هذه) اسم إشارة أشار به إلى الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن، والألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مُجملةٌ ، والأرجوزة : اسم للمفصَّل بابا فبابا ، فلم يحصل التطابق بين المبتدأ وخبره ، وقد عُلم أنَّ التطابق بينهما واجب. ومحصَّلُ ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب : أنَّ الكلام ليس على ظاهره، بل هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام على هذا ومفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) فحذف المضاف، وأقام المضاف إليه مقامه، ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ هذا الجواب مبني على تسليم أمرين: الأول: أنَّ الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن التي أشار إليها بـ(هذه) لا تكون إلا مجملة. أ والثاني: أنَّ لفظ (أرجوزة) اسم للمفصل بابا فبابا . ولك أن تجيب على هذا الاعتراض بجوابين آخرين غير جواب الشارح الذي أشارت إليه أنه قد يقوم كلمة (مفصل) ومحصل الجواب الأول منهما : أنا نمنع أنه لا يقوم بالذهن إلا المجمل، وتدعي بالذَّهن المفصَّل المنظَّم المرتب المتّسق ، وهذا هو مذهب أهل التحقيق. ومحصل الجواب الثاني منهما : أنا لا نسلّم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم للمفصل المرتب، بل يجوز الأقرب ؛ لأنه يبعد ملاحظتها عند الوضع بيتا أن تكون اسماً للهيئة المجملة ، بل هذا هو بيتا، وإذا أجبنا بأحد هذين الجوابين لم نكن في حاجة إلى تقدير مضاف . ومحصل الاعتراض الثاني: أنَّ المشار إليه بقوله (هذه) هو ما في ذهن المصنّف وحده؛ فهو جزئي ، والأرجوزة : اسم للألفاظ لا بقيد كونها في ذهن المصنف، بل أعمُّ مِن أنْ تكون في ذهنه أو في ذهن غيره ؛ فهو كلّي ، فلم يتطابق المبتدأ والخبر أيضاً. ومحصل ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب على هذا الاعتراض: أن الكلام ليس على ظاهره أيضًا، وإنَّما هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام (مفصل نوع هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) ، وأنت جد خبير أن هذا الجواب يتضمن تسليم ما ذكره المعترض في اعتراضه، ومنه أن الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره ، وهو مبني على أنَّ أسماء الكتب ونحوها من قبيل علم الجنس أو اسم الجنس، ولك أن تجيب بجواب آخر على هذا الاعتراض ، =
And the detailed kind of these words are the imaginative [words] indicating the intended meanings. (1) The commentator, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated by his saying: (And the detailed kind of these words [is] to repulse two objections that occur regarding the author's statement which is his saying: ('And this is an urjuzah (I have titled it...') The summary of the first objection is that his saying 'this' is a demonstrative pronoun referring to the words present in the mind, and the words present in the mind are summary (mujmal), while 'urjuzah' is a name for the detailed (mufassal) chapter by chapter, so there is no agreement between the subject and predicate, and it is known that agreement between them is obligatory. The summary of what the commentator indicated as an answer: that the speech is not according to its apparent meaning, but rather it is on the assumption of an omitted noun before the subject, and the original speech according to this is 'And the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah', so the noun was omitted and the genitive was put in its place, and it is not hidden from you that this answer is based on admitting two matters: first: that the words present in the mind referred to by 'these' are only summary; and second: that the word 'urjuzah' is a name for the detailed chapter by chapter. And you can answer this objection with two other answers besides the commentator's answer which he indicated: that the word 'detailed' (mufassal) may be [implicit]. The summary of the first of them: we deny that only the summary is present in the mind, and we claim that the detailed, organized, arranged, consistent [material] is present in the mind, and this is the doctrine of the people of verification. And the summary of the second of them: we do not concede that 'urjuzah' is a name for the detailed organized [poem], but rather the closer [meaning] is possible; because it is far-fetched to regard it when coined as a name for the summary form, but rather this is [the case]. And if we answer with either of these two answers, we have no need to assume an omitted noun. And the summary of the second objection: that the referent of his saying 'this' is only what is in the author's mind, so it is particular (juz'i), while 'urjuzah' is a name for the words without restriction of being in the author's mind, but more general than being in his mind or in someone else's, so it is universal (kulli), so the subject and predicate also do not agree. And the summary of what the commentator indicated as an answer to this objection: that the speech is not according to its apparent meaning either, but rather it is on the assumption of an omitted noun before the subject, and the original speech is 'the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah'. And you are fully aware that this answer involves conceding what the objector mentioned in his objection, including that 'urjuzah' is a name for what is in the author's mind and others, and it is based on that names of books and the like are of the type of generic proper noun (ism al-jins) or common noun (ism al-jins). And you can answer with another answer to this objection, =

Commentary
This passage is a detailed response to two grammatical/logical objections concerning the phrase 'وهذه أرجوزة' (And this is an urjuzah). The first objection argues that 'هذه' (this) refers to the summary words in the mind, while 'أرجوزة' denotes a detailed arrangement, causing a mismatch. The commentator's initial answer involves positing an omitted noun, making the underlying sentence 'مفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة' (the detailed kind of these words is an urjuzah). Two alternative answers are then presented: first, that the mind can contain detailed, organized material, not just summary; second, that 'أرجوزة' in its coinage may refer to a summary form, removing the need for an omitted noun. The second objection argues that 'هذه' refers only to the author's mind (particular), while 'أرجوزة' is general (universal). The commentator again resorts to an omitted noun, but this answer concedes the objector's point. Another answer is suggested (though not fully given). The passage demonstrates careful grammatical reasoning typical of kalam commentary.

Misconception: The second objection cannot be resolved because 'هذه' and 'أرجوزة' are inherently incompatible in particularity.
Correction: The commentator shows that by positing an ellipsis, the sentence can be made coherent, though it concedes the universal nature of 'urjuzah'.

The second objection to 'هذه أرجوزة' is resolved by an ellipsis that concedes the universality of 'urjuzah', with a hint that another answer may exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator conceded part of the objection to preserve the overall meaning. In your own life, are you willing to admit when an opponent has a valid point in a disagreement, even if it means adjusting your position? How does this lesson challenge your approach to debates about faith?
?
The distinction between particular and universal is subtle yet crucial in this argument. How often do you examine your own statements for logical precision? Commit to reviewing one belief you hold and testing whether it rests on a confusion of particular and universal claims.

First Objection to the Opening Line: Grammar and Meaning
First objection about mental vs. detailed words and three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first grammatical objection to the opening line of the poem and the three scholarly responses to it.

Core Idea
Grammatical objections can reveal deeper theological assumptions about language and meaning.

When scholars examine a poetic text, they often start with its grammar. The first objection to the opening line of this poem concerns the mismatch between 'these' (referring to summary mental words) and 'arjuzah' (a detailed poem). This lesson unpacks the objection and three distinct answers, each reflecting a different view on how language works in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and identify the problem
Read the passage once to grasp the objection: the demonstrative 'these' points to mental words (summary), while 'arjuzah' means detailed arrangement. There is a mismatch between subject and predicate.
Notice that the objector assumes mental words are only summary.
2
Examine the commentator's answer and its two assumptions
The commentator proposes that the sentence is elliptical: an implied word (mudaf) is omitted. This answer relies on two assumptions: mental words are only summary, and 'arjuzah' is detailed. Identify these assumptions.
If both assumptions hold, the ellipse resolves the mismatch.
3
Consider the two alternative answers
The author offers two further answers: first, that mental words can actually be detailed and organised, so no mismatch exists; second, that 'arjuzah' may refer to a summary form, not necessarily detailed. Each answer avoids the need for an ellipsis.
These answers reflect different views on mental representation and linguistic coinage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الاعتراض الأوّل أنَّ قولَه : (هذه) اسم إشارة أشار به إلى الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن، والألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مُجملةٌ ، والأرجوزة : اسم للمفصَّل بابا فبابا ، فلم يحصل التطابق بين المبتدأ وخبره ، وقد عُلم أنَّ التطابق بينهما واجب. ومحصَّلُ ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب : أنَّ الكلام ليس على ظاهره، بل هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام على هذا ومفصل هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) فحذف المضاف، وأقام المضاف إليه مقامه، ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ هذا الجواب مبني على تسليم أمرين: الأول: أنَّ الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن التي أشار إليها بـ(هذه) لا تكون إلا مجملة. أ والثاني: أنَّ لفظ (أرجوزة) اسم للمفصل بابا فبابا . ولك أن تجيب على هذا الاعتراض بجوابين آخرين غير جواب الشارح الذي أشارت إليه أنه قد يقوم كلمة (مفصل) ومحصل الجواب الأول منهما : أنا نمنع أنه لا يقوم بالذهن إلا المجمل، وتدعي بالذَّهن المفصَّل المنظَّم المرتب المتّسق ، وهذا هو مذهب أهل التحقيق. ومحصل الجواب الثاني منهما : أنا لا نسلّم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم للمفصل المرتب، بل يجوز الأقرب ؛ لأنه يبعد ملاحظتها عند الوضع بيتا أن تكون اسماً للهيئة المجملة ، بل هذا هو فبيتا، وإذا أجبنا بأحد هذين الجوابين لم نكن في حاجة إلى تقدير مضاف.
The summary of the first objection: that his statement (these) is a demonstrative pronoun referring to the words present in the mind, and the words present in the mind are summary (mujmal), while arjuzah is a name for the detailed (mufassal) chapter by chapter, so there is no correspondence between the subject and predicate, and it is known that correspondence between them is obligatory. The summary of what the commentator pointed to as the answer: that the speech is not literal, but rather it is with an implied word (mudaf) before the subject, and the original wording is 'and the detailed of these words is an arjuzah', so the mudaf was omitted and the mudaf ilayh was put in its place. And it is not hidden from you that this answer is based on accepting two things: first, that the words present in the mind which he indicated by 'these' are only summary; and second, that the word 'arjuzah' is a name for the detailed chapter by chapter. And you may answer this objection with two other answers besides the commentator's answer which he indicated. The summary of the first of them: we deny that only the summary occurs in the mind, and we claim that the detailed, organized, arranged, consistent [content] occurs in the mind, and this is the doctrine of the people of verification. The summary of the second of them: we do not concede that arjuzah is a name for the detailed arranged [structure], but rather it is permissible that it is a name for the summary form, because it is unlikely that at the time of coinage it was observed line by line. And if we answer with one of these two answers, we have no need to imply a mudaf.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the first grammatical objection to the opening line of the poem and then gives three possible answers. The objector notes that the demonstrative 'these' refers to mental words, which are summary, while 'arjuzah' refers to a detailed arrangement, so the subject and predicate don't match. The commentator's answer is that the sentence is elliptical: an implied word (mudaf) is omitted before the subject, so the true meaning is 'the detailed of these words is an arjuzah'. This answer assumes two things: mental words are only summary, and arjuzah names detailed arrangement. The passage then offers two alternative answers that avoid the ellipsis: first, that mental words can actually be detailed and organized; second, that arjuzah may refer to a summary form, not necessarily detailed. If either alternative is accepted, no ellipsis is needed.

Correction: The line is elliptical: an implied word (mudaf) is omitted, making it correct once restored. Alternatively, other answers show it can be understood without ellipsis.

D) The predicate is missing a verb.

Key Takeaway

The Second Objection and the Sharh's Response
Second objection and sharh's answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the line 'hadhihi arjuzah' and the sharh's proposed resolution by positing an implied word before the subject.

Core Idea
The second objection cracks the correspondence between subject and predicate by distinguishing particular vs. universal terms.

After resolving the first objection with an ellipsis, a second objection emerges: the demonstrative 'these' refers to a particular mental content, while 'arjuzah' is a universal term that could apply to anyone's words. This lesson presents the objection and the sharh's answer that again invokes an implied word, though this time with a concession about the term's universality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage that presents the second objection. Notice the distinction between particular (juz'i) and universal (kulli).
The objection argues that 'these' refers only to the author's mental words, while 'arjuzah' is a general term for any such composition.
2
Identify the mismatch
See why the subject and predicate do not correspond: one is particular, the other universal.
3
Examine the sharh's answer
Read the sharh's response: he posits an implied word before the subject, so the intended meaning is 'the detailed type of these words is an arjuzah.'
Note that this answer concedes that 'arjuzah' is universal, which may be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحصل الاعتراض الثاني: أنَّ المشار إليه بقوله (هذه) هو ما في ذهن المصنّف وحده؛ فهو جزئي ، والأرجوزة : اسم للألفاظ لا بقيد كونها في ذهن المصنف، بل أعمُّ مِن أنْ تكون في ذهنه أو في ذهن غيره ؛ فهو كلّي ، فلم يتطابق المبتدأ والخبر أيضاً. ومحصل ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب على هذا الاعتراض: أن الكلام ليس على ظاهره أيضًا، وإنَّما هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام (مفصل نوع هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) ، وأنت جد خبير أن هذا الجواب يتضمن تسليم ما ذكره المعترض في اعتراضه، ومنه أن الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره ، وهو مبني على أنَّ أسماء الكتب ونحوها من قبيل علم الجنس أو اسم الجنس، ولك أن تجيب بجواب آخر على هذا الاعتراض، = ۱۳۲ وحاصله: أنا لا نسلم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره؛ لأن هذا أسماء الكتب من قبيل أعلام الأجناس ، أو أسماء الأجناس ، ونحن لا نقول به ، بل الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وحده بناء على أنَّ أسماء الكتب من قبيل الأعلام الشخصية، وهو ما نختاره. بقي هنا شيء، وهو أَنَّا لا نرتضي جواب الشارح على الاعتراضين؛ حتى على فرض تسليم كل ما جاء في كلام المعترض ، وذلك لأنه جعل تقدير المضاف قبل المبتدأ، والذي نختاره على هذا الفرض أن نجعل تقدير المضاف قبل الخبر؛ فيكون التقدير: (وهذه الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مجمل جنس أرجوزة... الخ) لأنَّ التقدير في الأول يكون بمنزلة نزع الخُف قبل الوصول لشاطئ النهر كما ذكره المحقق الخَيَّالي ، فإنَّ أول الكلام وقع موقعه ، ولم يأت الاعتراض إلا بسبب آخره ؛ فليكن التقدير في آخره. محي.
The summary of the second objection: that the referent of his statement (these) is what is in the mind of the author alone, so it is particular (juzi), while arjuzah is a name for words not restricted to being in the author's mind, but more general: either in his mind or in another's; so it is universal (kulli), and thus the subject and predicate do not correspond. The summary of what the commentator pointed to as the answer to this objection: that the speech is not literal either, but rather it has an implied word before the subject, and the original wording is 'the detailed type of these words is an arjuzah'. And you are well aware that this answer involves conceding what the objector stated in his objection, including that arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind and others', and it is based on the view that names of books and the like are of the type of common nouns (jins) or generic nouns. And you may answer this objection with another answer: its summary is: we do not concede that arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind and others', because these names of books are of the type of proper nouns for genera (a'lam al-ajnas) or generic nouns, and we do not say that, but rather arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind alone, based on the view that names of books are of the type of personal proper nouns (a'lam shakhsiyya), and that is what we choose. There remains a point: that we do not approve of the commentator's answer to the two objections, even assuming everything in the objector's speech is granted, because he placed the implied word before the subject, while what we choose on this assumption is to place the implied word before the predicate, so the estimation would be: 'these words present in the mind are the summary type of arjuzah...' etc., because the estimation in the first [position] is like removing the shoe before reaching the riverbank, as the investigator al-Khayyali mentioned. For the beginning of the speech is in its proper place, and the objection only came due to its end; so let the estimation be at its end. Muhyi.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the same line, then gives the sharh's answer and two further responses. The objection is that 'these' refers to the author's own mental words (particular), while 'arjuzah' is a universal term that could apply to anyone's words, so again no correspondence. The sharh's answer again posits an ellipsis before the subject, but the commentator notes that this concedes the objection's point about the term's universality. The commentator then gives his own alternative: he denies that arjuzah is universal, arguing that book names are personal proper nouns, so the term only refers to the author's own words, thus the subject and predicate both refer to the same particular. Finally, the commentator critiques the sharh's approach: even if the objection's premises are accepted, the ellipsis should be placed before the predicate, not the subject, because the problem is at the end of the sentence, not the beginning. He illustrates this with the metaphor of removing a shoe before crossing a river.

Key Terms
جزئي
كلي
تقدير مضاف
علم الجنس
اسم الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ellipsis perfectly resolves the objection without any concession.
Correction: The sharh's answer concedes that 'arjuzah' is universal, which contradicts the particularity of the subject and does not fully resolve the mismatch.

Checkpoint
What does the sharh's answer concede regarding the term 'arjuzah'?
A) It is a particular term referring only to the author's mind.
B) It is a universal term applicable to any mind.
C) It is a proper noun for books.
D) It is an ambiguous term.

Key Takeaway
The sharh's answer resolves the grammar but at the cost of conceding that 'arjuzah' is a universal term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a position while conceding a point that undermined it, like the sharh conceded 'arjuzah' is universal? What does that reveal about your priorities—truth or winning the argument?
?
The objector insisted on a precise match between subject and predicate. In your own life, are you holding everyone to a standard of precision that you yourself do not meet? Where can you show more grace?
?
When you reinterpret a belief to avoid an inconsistency (like adding an implied word), are you honestly facing the objection or merely sidestepping it? Ask Allah for sincerity in your convictions.

The Commentator's Alternative: Book Names as Personal Proper Nouns
Commentator's alternative answer denying universality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's preferred resolution to the second objection by denying that 'arjuzah' is a universal term and instead considering book names as personal proper nouns.

Core Idea
Book names are personal proper nouns, so 'arjuzah' refers only to the author's own words.

Instead of conceding the objection's assumption that 'arjuzah' is universal, the commentator offers a bold alternative: deny that 'arjuzah' is a universal term at all. By classifying book names as personal proper nouns, the term exclusively designates the author's own verbal composition. This preserves the particularity of both subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption rejected
The commentator first rejects the assumption that 'arjuzah' names words in any mind. He argues that book names are not generic.
This directly challenges the objector's premise.
2
See the classification of book names
The commentator explains that book names are personal proper nouns (a'lam shakhsiyya), not common or generic nouns.
This is the key move that preserves particularity.
3
Grasp why this resolves the objection
Since 'arjuzah' is a personal proper noun, it refers only to the author's particular composition, matching the particularity of 'these'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحصل الاعتراض الثاني: أنَّ المشار إليه بقوله (هذه) هو ما في ذهن المصنّف وحده؛ فهو جزئي ، والأرجوزة : اسم للألفاظ لا بقيد كونها في ذهن المصنف، بل أعمُّ مِن أنْ تكون في ذهنه أو في ذهن غيره ؛ فهو كلّي ، فلم يتطابق المبتدأ والخبر أيضاً. ومحصل ما أشار إليه الشارح من الجواب على هذا الاعتراض: أن الكلام ليس على ظاهره أيضًا، وإنَّما هو على تقدير مضاف قبل المبتدأ، وأصل الكلام (مفصل نوع هذه الألفاظ أرجوزة) ، وأنت جد خبير أن هذا الجواب يتضمن تسليم ما ذكره المعترض في اعتراضه، ومنه أن الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره ، وهو مبني على أنَّ أسماء الكتب ونحوها من قبيل علم الجنس أو اسم الجنس، ولك أن تجيب بجواب آخر على هذا الاعتراض، = ۱۳۲ وحاصله: أنا لا نسلم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره؛ لأن هذا أسماء الكتب من قبيل أعلام الأجناس ، أو أسماء الأجناس ، ونحن لا نقول به ، بل الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وحده بناء على أنَّ أسماء الكتب من قبيل الأعلام الشخصية، وهو ما نختاره. بقي هنا شيء، وهو أَنَّا لا نرتضي جواب الشارح على الاعتراضين؛ حتى على فرض تسليم كل ما جاء في كلام المعترض ، وذلك لأنه جعل تقدير المضاف قبل المبتدأ، والذي نختاره على هذا الفرض أن نجعل تقدير المضاف قبل الخبر؛ فيكون التقدير: (وهذه الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مجمل جنس أرجوزة... الخ) لأنَّ التقدير في الأول يكون بمنزلة نزع الخُف قبل الوصول لشاطئ النهر كما ذكره المحقق الخَيَّالي ، فإنَّ أول الكلام وقع موقعه ، ولم يأت الاعتراض إلا بسبب آخره ؛ فليكن التقدير في آخره. محي.
The summary of the second objection: that the referent of his statement (these) is what is in the mind of the author alone, so it is particular (juzi), while arjuzah is a name for words not restricted to being in the author's mind, but more general: either in his mind or in another's; so it is universal (kulli), and thus the subject and predicate do not correspond. The summary of what the commentator pointed to as the answer to this objection: that the speech is not literal either, but rather it has an implied word before the subject, and the original wording is 'the detailed type of these words is an arjuzah'. And you are well aware that this answer involves conceding what the objector stated in his objection, including that arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind and others', and it is based on the view that names of books and the like are of the type of common nouns (jins) or generic nouns. And you may answer this objection with another answer: its summary is: we do not concede that arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind and others', because these names of books are of the type of proper nouns for genera (a'lam al-ajnas) or generic nouns, and we do not say that, but rather arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind alone, based on the view that names of books are of the type of personal proper nouns (a'lam shakhsiyya), and that is what we choose. There remains a point: that we do not approve of the commentator's answer to the two objections, even assuming everything in the objector's speech is granted, because he placed the implied word before the subject, while what we choose on this assumption is to place the implied word before the predicate, so the estimation would be: 'these words present in the mind are the summary type of arjuzah...' etc., because the estimation in the first [position] is like removing the shoe before reaching the riverbank, as the investigator al-Khayyali mentioned. For the beginning of the speech is in its proper place, and the objection only came due to its end; so let the estimation be at its end. Muhyi.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the same line, then gives the sharh's answer and two further responses. The objection is that 'these' refers to the author's own mental words (particular), while 'arjuzah' is a universal term that could apply to anyone's words, so again no correspondence. The sharh's answer again posits an ellipsis before the subject, but the commentator notes that this concedes the objection's point about the term's universality. The commentator then gives his own alternative: he denies that arjuzah is universal, arguing that book names are personal proper nouns, so the term only refers to the author's own words, thus the subject and predicate both refer to the same particular. Finally, the commentator critiques the sharh's approach: even if the objection's premises are accepted, the ellipsis should be placed before the predicate, not the subject, because the problem is at the end of the sentence, not the beginning. He illustrates this with the metaphor of removing a shoe before crossing a river.

Key Terms
أعلام الأجناس
أسماء الأجناس
الأعلام الشخصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator classify book names in his preferred answer?
A) As common nouns.
B) As proper nouns for genera.
C) As personal proper nouns.
D) As generic nouns.

Key Takeaway
By classifying book names as personal proper nouns, the commentator avoids the particular-universal mismatch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last opened a book of Islamic scholarship, did you approach its title as a generic label or as the unique expression of its author? How would your reading change if you treated every book title as a personal proper noun pointing to a singular mind?
?
The commentator makes a deliberate choice to avoid a logical mismatch. In your own theological studies, have you ever assumed a term was universal when it was actually specific? How might that assumption have affected your understanding of a scholar's intention?
?
The classification of 'arjuzah' as personal forces you to see it as belonging to one author alone. Think of a sacred text you rely on—do you treat it as a generic truth available to all, or as a personal address from the author to you? If it is personal, what responsibility does that place on you today?

Critiquing the Sharh: Where to Place the Implied Word
Critique of sharh's answer regarding ellipsis placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique that the sharh's ellipsis should be placed before the predicate rather than the subject, using the riverbank metaphor.

Core Idea
The ellipsis should fix the end of the sentence, not the beginning.

Even if the objection's premises are accepted, the commentator finds fault with the sharh's placement of the implied word. He argues that the beginning of the sentence is fine; the mismatch only arises at the end. Therefore, the ellipsis should modify the predicate, not the subject. He illustrates this with the metaphor of removing a shoe before crossing a river.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the critique
The commentator states he does not approve of the sharh's answer even if all premises are granted, because the ellipsis is placed before the subject instead of the predicate.
The critique is about the location of the implied word.
2
Understand the riverbank metaphor
The metaphor compares placing the ellipsis before the subject to removing a shoe before reaching the riverbank—it's premature. The problem is at the end, so fix the end.
Al-Khayyali is cited for this metaphor.
3
See the preferred placement
The commentator prefers to place the implied word before the predicate, so the estimation becomes 'these words present in the mind are the summary type of arjuzah...'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Author's Prayer for Acceptance and Reward
The author prays for acceptance and defines hope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's prayer for acceptance and reward and the meaning of hope (رجاء) in Islamic ethics.

Core Idea
The author begins by placing his work in Allah's hands, seeking acceptance and reward.

This opening passage shows the author's humility and reliance on Allah. He prays for his own and his reader's benefit, explaining that hope involves both the heart and action. Let's read the original Arabic and its translation.

Guided Path
1
Read the prayer and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that the author prays for acceptance (قبول) and reward (ثواب) for himself and the student.
The repetition of 'والله أرجو' emphasizes sincerity.
2
Understand the definition of hope (رجاء)
The author defines hope as the heart's attachment to something desired in the future, combined with taking the necessary means. This is a key concept in Islamic spirituality.
Hope is not mere wishful thinking; it requires action.
3
Reflect on why the author begins with prayer
Consider the purpose of starting a theological work with a prayer. It sets a tone of humility and reliance on Divine acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودعاء المصنّف لنفسه ولمُتعلّم هذه المنظومة
والله أَرْجُو فِي القَبُول نَافِعًا بها مُرِيْدًا فِي الثَّوَابِ طَامِعا
وَاللهَ أَرْجُو فِي حُصول القَبُولِ، والرَّجَاءُ عُرفًا: تَعلَّق القلب بمرغوب في حصوله في المستقبل، مع الأخذ في أسباب الحصول.
إن قلت: إنّ مدحَ المصنّف كتابه بأنَّه منقح مصفى من الشبه والعقائد الفاسدة خالٍ من الحشو والتطويل ليس فيه إلَّا لُباب العلم وخالصه ، كل ذلك خلاف الأولى وداخل تحت ما نهى الله تعالى عنه بقوله: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [النجم: 32].
أجيب عن ذلك : بأنَّه لم يقصد بهذا الكلام تزكية نفسه ولا تزكية كتابه ، وإنما قصد النُّصح للمسلمين بأن يتعاطوا فهمه وحفظه ليكونوا بمنجاة من خطر التقليد، على أَنَّا لو سَلَّمنا أنَّ مقصوده مدح كتابه ومدح نفسه ؛ لا نُسلّم أنه داخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [النجم: 32] ، بل هو من قبيل التّحدث بنعمة الله تعالى عليه المأذون فيه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [الضحى: 11] ، ومدح المرء نفسه جائز في مواضع.
And the author's prayer for himself and for the student of this poem: And God I hope for acceptance, benefiting thereby one who desires, hoping for reward. And God I hope for obtaining acceptance, and hope conventionally is the heart's attachment to something desired to be obtained in the future, while taking the means of obtaining. If you say: The author's praising his book as refined, purified from doubts and corrupt beliefs, free from padding and prolixity, containing only the core and pure substance of knowledge, all that is contrary to what is proper and falls under what God Almighty forbade by His saying: 'Do not praise yourselves' [An-Najm: 32]. The answer is: He did not intend by this speech to praise himself nor to praise his book, but rather he intended advice to Muslims that they engage in understanding and memorizing it to be safe from the danger of blind imitation. Even if we assume that his intention was to praise his book and praise himself, we do not accept that it falls under the verse 'Do not praise yourselves'; rather it is of the type of recounting God's blessing upon him, which is permitted by His saying: 'And as for the favor of your Lord, proclaim' [Ad-Duha: 11], and self-praise is permissible in certain situations.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage contains the author's prayer asking God for acceptance and reward for himself and the student. It then addresses an objection that praising one's own book amounts to self-praise forbidden in the Qur'an. The answer gives two defenses: first, the author's intention was sincere advice to Muslims, not self-praise; second, even if considered self-praise, it falls under the permissible category of recounting God's blessings, as commanded in Surah Ad-Duha. Therefore, the author's commendation of his work is justified.

Key Terms
دعاء
رجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wishful thinking without effort
B) Heart's attachment with taking means
C) Certainty of reward
D) Despair of Allah's mercy

Key Takeaway
True hope combines heartfelt longing with active pursuit of means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson defines hope as attaching your heart to something desired while taking means. Look at your greatest hope in life—does it match this definition? If not, what is missing?
?
How often do you pray for acceptance and reward like the author? When was the last time you sincerely asked Allah to accept your deeds? This very moment, make that du'ā'.

Defending Against the Charge of Self-Praise
The author responds to an objection about self-praise with two defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's defense against the charge that praising his own book constitutes forbidden self-praise.

Core Idea
The author's praise of his work is either sincere advice or recounting Allah's blessings.

The author anticipates an objection based on the Qur'anic prohibition of self-praise. He provides two powerful responses. This passage illustrates a key principle in Islamic scholarship: praising one's own work can be permissible when the intention is sincere advice or gratitude to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that the author's praise of his book as refined and free of flaws constitutes forbidden self-praise (تزكية النفس), citing verse 32 of Surah An-Najm.
The objector views the praise as contrary to proper conduct.
2
Study the first answer
The author's first defense: his intention was not self-praise but sincere advice (نصح) to Muslims to learn and memorize the book to avoid blind imitation.
Intention is key in Islamic ethics.
3
Study the second answer
Even if one insists it is self-praise, the author argues it is actually recounting Allah's blessing (التحدث بنعمة الله), which is commanded in Surah Ad-Duha.
This shifts the framework from prohibition to obligation.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider that self-praise is permissible in certain situations, especially when it is actually gratitude or advice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَاللهَ أَرْجُو فِي حُصول القَبُولِ، والرَّجَاءُ عُرفًا: تَعلَّق القلب بمرغوب في حصوله في المستقبل، مع الأخذ في أسباب الحصول.
إن قلت: إنّ مدحَ المصنّف كتابه بأنَّه منقح مصفى من الشبه والعقائد الفاسدة خالٍ من الحشو والتطويل ليس فيه إلَّا لُباب العلم وخالصه ، كل ذلك خلاف الأولى وداخل تحت ما نهى الله تعالى عنه بقوله: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [النجم: 32].
أجيب عن ذلك : بأنَّه لم يقصد بهذا الكلام تزكية نفسه ولا تزكية كتابه ، وإنما قصد النُّصح للمسلمين بأن يتعاطوا فهمه وحفظه ليكونوا بمنجاة من خطر التقليد، على أَنَّا لو سَلَّمنا أنَّ مقصوده مدح كتابه ومدح نفسه ؛ لا نُسلّم أنه داخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [النجم: 32] ، بل هو من قبيل التّحدث بنعمة الله تعالى عليه المأذون فيه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [الضحى: 11] ، ومدح المرء نفسه جائز في مواضع.
And the author's prayer for himself and for the student of this poem: And God I hope for acceptance, benefiting thereby one who desires, hoping for reward. And God I hope for obtaining acceptance, and hope conventionally is the heart's attachment to something desired to be obtained in the future, while taking the means of obtaining. If you say: The author's praising his book as refined, purified from doubts and corrupt beliefs, free from padding and prolixity, containing only the core and pure substance of knowledge, all that is contrary to what is proper and falls under what God Almighty forbade by His saying: 'Do not praise yourselves' [An-Najm: 32]. The answer is: He did not intend by this speech to praise himself nor to praise his book, but rather he intended advice to Muslims that they engage in understanding and memorizing it to be safe from the danger of blind imitation. Even if we assume that his intention was to praise his book and praise himself, we do not accept that it falls under the verse 'Do not praise yourselves'; rather it is of the type of recounting God's blessing upon him, which is permitted by His saying: 'And as for the favor of your Lord, proclaim' [Ad-Duha: 11], and self-praise is permissible in certain situations.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage contains the author's prayer asking God for acceptance and reward for himself and the student. It then addresses an objection that praising one's own book amounts to self-praise forbidden in the Qur'an. The answer gives two defenses: first, the author's intention was sincere advice to Muslims, not self-praise; second, even if considered self-praise, it falls under the permissible category of recounting God's blessings, as commanded in Surah Ad-Duha. Therefore, the author's commendation of his work is justified.

Key Terms
تزكية
نصح
التحدث بنعمة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any self-praise is always forbidden.
Correction: The passage shows that if intention is advice or recounting blessings, it is permissible.

Checkpoint
What is the second defense regarding the author's praise?
A) It is false
B) It is recounting Allah's blessing
C) It is only for scholars
D) It is mandatory

Key Takeaway
Praising one's own work can be justified if the intention is sincere advice or gratitude to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about your own accomplishments, do you pause to examine your intention? Are you seeking validation, or do you genuinely intend to benefit others through sincere advice?
?
Have you ever hidden a blessing Allah has given you—like knowledge, a skill, or an opportunity—because you feared being seen as boastful? How does this passage challenge that false humility?
?
Think of a time you criticized someone else's self-praise. Now, with this lesson in mind, could you have been reacting to your own discomfort rather than their intention? Will you now give others the benefit of the doubt?

Defending the Author's Praise of His Own Work
The commentator defends the author's praise of his own work against the charge of self-praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the author's self-praise as sincere advice and gratitude for blessings, not prohibited self-glorification.

Core Idea
Not all praise of one's own work is forbidden; intention and context matter.

This passage addresses a common objection: does the author's praise of his own book amount to prohibited self-praise? The commentator offers a two-part defense, clarifying that the intention was sincere advice and that even if taken as praise, it falls under speaking of God's blessings. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: first the objection, then the answer split into two levels.
2
Analyze the first level of the answer
The commentator states the author's true intention was sincere advice (النصح) to Muslims, not self-glorification. Understand how intention changes the ruling.
The proof is that the author wanted people to benefit, not to boast.
3
Analyze the second level of the answer
Even if we assume the intention was praise, the commentator argues it is not prohibited but rather a permissible form of speaking about God's blessings (التحدث بنعمة الله). Self-praise is allowed in certain contexts.
The Quranic verse about speaking blessings is cited as support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت: إنّ مدح المصنّف كتابه بأنَّه منقح مصفى من الشبه والعقائد الفاسدة خالٍ من الحشو والتطويل ليس فيه إلَّا لُباب العلم وخالصه ، كل ذلك خلاف الأولى وداخل تحت ما نهى الله تعالى عنه بقوله: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [النجم: ۳۲]. أجيب عن ذلك : بأنَّه لم يقصد بهذا الكلام تزكية نفسه ولا تزكية كتابه ، وإنما قصد النُّصح للمسلمين بأن يتعاطوا فهمه وحفظه ليكونوا بمنجاة من خطر التقليد، على أَنَّا لو سَلَّمنا أنَّ مقصوده مدح كتابه ومدح نفسه ؛ لا نُسلّم أنه داخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ [ النجم : ۳۲] ، بل هو من قبيل التّحدث بنعمة الله تعالى عليه المأذون فيه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [الضحى: ۱۱] ، ومدح المرء نفسه جائز في مواضع. محي.
If you say: Indeed the author's praise of his book as refined, purified from doubts and corrupt beliefs, free from redundancy and prolixity, containing nothing but the core of knowledge and its purest, all that is contrary to what is proper and falls under what Allah forbade in His saying: 'Do not praise yourselves' [53:32]. The answer to that: He did not intend by this statement to praise himself nor to praise his book, but rather he intended sincere advice to Muslims that they should engage in understanding and memorizing it to be safe from the danger of blind imitation. Moreover, even if we concede that his intention was to praise his book and praise himself, we do not concede that it falls under the verse 'Do not praise yourselves'; rather, it is from the category of speaking about the blessing of Allah upon him, which is permitted in His saying: 'And as for the favor of your Lord, report it' [93:11], and a person praising himself is permissible in certain contexts. End.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is that the author's description of his own book as refined and pure amounts to self-praise, which is prohibited in the Quran. The answer refutes this on two levels: first, the author's intention was not self-praise but sincere advice to the community, encouraging them to benefit from the work. Second, even if it were praise, it is not prohibited self-praise but rather an expression of gratitude for God's blessing, as permitted in the Quran, and self-praise is allowed in certain contexts.

مدح

التحدث بنعمة الله

Misconception: Any praise of one's own work is automatically forbidden self-praise.
Correction: The commentator shows that intention (sincere advice) and context (speaking about blessings) can make such praise permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the primary intention behind the author's praise of his book?
A) To boast about his own knowledge.
B) To encourage Muslims to benefit from the book.
C) To fulfill a requirement of the genre.
D) To criticize other scholars.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of praising one's own work depends on the intention and context; it can be a form of sincere advice and gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you share your successes or achievements, do you examine your intention to ensure it is sincere advice for others rather than self-glorification? How can you cultivate that intention starting today?
?
If someone accused you of self-praise, would your heart react with defense or with a genuine desire to clarify your intention? What does this lesson teach you about checking your own motives before responding?
?
Are you willing to let your work—even if praised—be a means of benefit for others, even if it appears as self-praise? What step can you take this week to use your accomplishments to sincerely advise others?
