The Prohibition on Investigating the Soul is for Commendation
Argument that the prohibition on investigating the soul is for commendation, citing Maliki practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses the Maliki scholars' practice as evidence that the prohibition on investigating the soul is for commendation (tanzih) rather than absolute forbiddance.

Core Idea
The Maliki school's engagement shows the prohibition is commendation, not prohibition.

In this passage, the author tackles a crucial methodological point: how to interpret prophetic prohibitions on theological investigation. He presents an argument from established practice, showing that the very scholars who transmitted the prohibition themselves engaged in the discussion, indicating a nuanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the author's argument.
The passage uses technical terms like sanad and musnad.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the author's core claim: the prohibition is for commendation, and the evidence is the Maliki scholars' engagement.
Notice the structure: if the prohibition were absolute, great scholars would not have participated.
3
Understand the role of the Maliki school
Reflect on why the author specifically cites the Maliki school. Their practice serves as a transmitted proof (naql) that the ruling is tanzih.
The Maliki school is known for its adherence to the practice of the people of Medina.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument strengthens the permissibility of kalam as a discipline, provided it is done with proper methodology.
This is a key methodological point in classical Sunni theology.

For the text came from them with this chain (sanad) which is the path leading to the text; here musnad is used, meaning: if delving into it were forbidden, such great ones would not have undertaken it.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is an argument supporting the permissibility of discussing the reality of the soul. The author first notes the section heading on sam'iyyat (matters known through revelation). He then addresses those who cite a prohibition from Ahl al-Sunnah against delving into the soul's reality. He says: suffice for you as evidence that the prohibition is for commendation (tanzih) not categorical forbiddance is the fact that the scholars of the Maliki school themselves engaged in such discussion. Since reliable texts (nass) came from them with authentic chains (sanad), the word musnad is used here to mean the chain. The argument is that if such investigation were truly impermissible, great imams like those of the Maliki school would not have undertaken it. This reasoning uses their practice as a proof that the prohibition is not absolute.

Belief in Sam‘iyyat: Rational Possibility, Revelatory Obligation
Author explains epistemological status of grave matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why matters of the unseen (like grave punishment) are only rationally possible but obligatory to believe due to revelation.

Core Idea
Revelation makes obligatory what reason leaves possible.

This lesson clarifies the delicate relationship between reason and revelation in Islamic theology. The author distinguishes between what reason can deem possible and what revelation makes binding. The passage focuses on the three matters of the grave, establishing that while they are not rationally necessary, they become obligatory upon authentic prophetic report.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing sink in.
The passage is short but dense with technical terms.
2
Identify the two judgments
Notice the author says each matter is 'jā’iz ‘aqlan' (rationally permissible) and 'wājib sam‘an' (obligatory by revelation). Contrast these two categories.
This is the core distinction of the lesson.
3
Understand why obligation follows
The author gives the reason: the matter is rationally possible and reported by the truthful one (al-Ṣādiq). Reflect on how prophetic truthfulness transfers possibility into obligation.
The key term is 'al-Ṣādiq' – the infallible informant.
4
Note the consensus mentioned
The author states that this view is held by Ahl al-Sunnah and the majority of Mu‘tazila. This shows wide agreement on the principle.
Even theological opponents agree on this methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله (وَاجِبْ) أي: ثابت سمعا، خبر «سؤالنا» وما عطف عليه، أي: كلُّ واحدٍ مِن الثَّلاثة المذكورة جائز عقلا واجب سمعا؛ لأنَّه أمر ممكن عقلا أخبر به الصَّادقُ على ما نطقت به النُّصوص، وكُلُّ ما هو كذلك فهو حقٌّ يجب قبوله شرعًا، على هذا أهلُ السُّنَّة وجمهور المعتزلة.
And His saying 'wājib' means: established by revelation (sam‘an), as the predicate of 'our question' and what is conjoined with it. That is: each one of the three mentioned is permissible by reason (jā’iz ‘aqlan) and obligatory by revelation (wājib sam‘an), because it is a matter possible by reason, reported by the truthful one (al-Ṣādiq) according to what the texts have stated. And everything that is like that is true (ḥaqq) and must be accepted legally. Upon this are the Ahl al-Sunnah and the majority of the Mu‘tazila.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage clarifies the epistemological status of the three matters mentioned earlier (likely questioning, punishment, and reward in the grave). The author explains that the word 'wājib' in the text indicates that these matters are 'established by revelation' (sam‘an), not by reason. They are rationally possible (jā’iz ‘aqlan) but not rationally necessary; their obligation to believe comes from the reports of the truthful Prophet (al-Ṣādiq), which are conveyed through the revealed texts. Because these reports are from an infallible source, they are true and must be accepted legally (shar‘an). This position, the author notes, is held by the Ahl al-Sunnah and the majority of the Mu‘tazila, indicating broad agreement on the necessity of accepting authentic revelation regarding the unseen.

Correction: The author clarifies that these matters are only rationally possible; their obligation comes solely from revelation via the truthful Prophet.

Checkpoint

C) Because the majority of Muslims agree

Key Takeaway
Matters of the unseen are true and obligatory because they are reported by the truthful Prophet, even though reason only deems them possible.

?

The Detailed View: When to Trust and When to Acquire
Al-Bayjuuri enumerates arguments for kasb and tawakkul, then proposes a detailed view based on individual states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars ultimately favored a detailed view that determines whether acquisition or trust is preferable based on an individual's state and circumstances.

Core Idea
Your state decides: trust or acquisition — it's not one-size-fits-all.

Al-Bayjuuri presents two competing perspectives: some scholars prioritize acquisition (kasb) because it enables charity and self-sufficiency, while others prioritize trust (tawakkul) because it protects from worldly distractions. Yet neither absolute preference satisfies all cases. The correct position, he argues, is to adopt a detailed view (tafsil) based on the individual's readiness and circumstances. In this lesson, we will study the reasoning for both sides and then learn how the detailed view applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage along with its literal translation to get familiar with the author's arguments.
The passage is slightly truncated at the end, but the main ideas are complete.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that the passage first gives reasons for preferring acquisition (kasb) and then for preferring trust (tawakkul).
Each side has its own merits.
3
Understand the detailed view
The poet then states that the preponderant view is the detailed one: preference depends on the person's state.
An example is given when trust is favored: one who trusts without resentment and has no dependents.
4
Apply the criterion
Consider the example: for whom is tawakkul more preponderant? The answer involves inner state (no resentment) and external circumstances (no dependents).
This shows the detailed view is not arbitrary but based on concrete indicators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ تفصيل الخلاف في ترجيح التوكل أو الاكتساب]
فربَّحَ قومُ الأوَّل ؛ لما فيه مِن كَفِّ التَّطلُّع إلى ما في أيدي النَّاس ف النفس عن ومنعها مِن الخُضوع لهم والتَّذلُّل بين أيديهم، مع حيازة منصب التوسعة على عباد الله الله ، ومُواساة المحتاجين وصلة الأرحام بتوفيق الله تعالى. ورجح قوم (١) الثَّاني ؛ لما فيه من ترك كلِّ ما يُشغِلُ عن الله تعالى، وحيازة مقام السَّلامة من فتنة المال أو المُحاسبة عليه، والاتّصافِ بالرّغبة إلى الله تعالى والوثوق بما عنده .
(1) قوله: ( ورجح (قوم) كأبي جعفر الطبري . الشنواني.
[ تفصيل الخلاف في ترجيح التوكل أو الاكتساب]
ولما لم يكن هذا الإطلاقُ مَرضيًّا أشار إليه بقوله (والرَّاجِعُ التَّفصِيلُ) أي: القول به هو المختار عند القوم (۲) ، وأنّهما يختلفان باختلاف أحوال النَّاس، فمن يكون في توكَّله لا يَتسَخَطُ (۳) عند ضيق معيشته ; ولا يتطلعُ لسؤالِ أحدٍ ولا تتعلَّق به نفقة لازمة لمن لا يرضى بحاله ; فالتّوكُل فالتَّوكُل في حقه أرجحُ ; لما فيه مِن مجاهدة النفس على ترك شهواتها ولذاتها والصبر على
[Detail of the disagreement in preferring trust or acquisition]
A group considered the first (acquisition) superior; because it contains restraining the soul from looking towards what is in people's hands and preventing it from humbling itself before them and abasing itself in their presence, along with obtaining the status of being expansive towards God's servants, and comforting the needy, and joining kinship ties by God's success. And a group (1) considered the second (trust) superior; because it contains leaving everything that distracts from God, obtaining the station of safety from the trial of wealth or being called to account for it, and being characterized by desire for God and confidence in what He possesses.
(1) His saying: 'and a group considered (it) superior', like Abu Ja'far al-Tabari. Al-Shinnawi.
[Detail of the disagreement in preferring trust or acquisition]
And since this absolute (statement) was not satisfactory, he pointed to it by his saying: 'and the preponderant is the detailed view' i.e. the statement by it is the chosen one among the people (2), and that they differ according to the different states of people. So whoever is in his trust does not become resentful (3) at the straitness of his livelihood, nor does he aspire to ask anyone, nor is there a necessary expense attached to him for one who is not satisfied with his state; then trust, trust in his case is more preponderant; because it contains struggling the soul to abandon its desires and pleasures and patience upon
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments for preferring acquisition (kasb) over trust (tawakkul) and vice versa, and then introduces the detailed view (tafsil) as the preponderant position. The author first states that some people considered the first (acquisition) superior for several reasons: it restrains the soul from coveting what others possess, prevents humbling oneself before people, allows one to be expansive towards God's servants by helping the needy and maintaining family ties, all with God's success. Others considered trust superior because it involves leaving everything that distracts from God, achieving safety from the trials of wealth and accountability, and fostering desire for God and trust in His provision. The author then notes that this absolute preference (either for kasb alone or tawakkul alone) was not satisfactory, so the poem's line 'and the preponderant is the detailed view' indicates the chosen position: the two differ according to people's states. He gives an example: if a person's trust is such that he does not become resentful at tight circumstances, does not ask others, and has no dependents who are dissatisfied with his state, then trust is more preponderant for him because it involves struggling against the soul's desires and cultivating patience. The passage ends mid-sentence, implying further conditions for when acquisition might be preferred.

مجاملة النفس (mujāhada al-nafs)
التفصيل (al-tafsīl)
اليسخط (al-yaskhaṭ)
السلامة من فتنة المال (al-salāma min fitnat al-māl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that tawakkul (reliance on God) is always superior to earning a living.
Correction: The detailed view shows that trust is only better for those who can maintain it without resentment and have no dependents; otherwise, acquisition may be better.

Checkpoint
According to the detailed view, when is tawakkul considered more preponderant?
A) When the person has many dependents
B) When the person does not become resentful at straitness and does not need to ask others
C) When the person is wealthy
D) When the person is engaged in trade

Key Takeaway
The detailed view (tafsil) teaches that the choice between acquisition and trust is not absolute but depends on whether one can maintain trust without resentment and on one's responsibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your livelihood becomes tight, do you find resentment rising in your heart against God's decree? What does that reveal about the state of your trust? This week, whenever you feel that resentment, stop and recite 'Hasbunallahu wa ni'mal wakeel' and truly mean it.
?
Are you holding onto acquisition out of fear of poverty or a desire to control, or out of genuine responsibility? This lesson calls you to examine your motive. For one area of your life where you are most anxious about provision, commit to acting as if God alone is the Provider and see how your heart changes.
?
Consider a dependent in your life who is not satisfied with your state. Does that pressure you toward acquisition or away from trust? According to the detailed view, that very pressure might tip the scale. Are you willing to endure their dissatisfaction for the sake of deepening your reliance on Allah?

Enjoining Good and Forbidding Evil as a Communal Obligation
Declares al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar as a communal obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar is a communal obligation (fard kifayah) and its connection to the imamate.

Core Idea
Enjoining good and forbidding evil is a communal duty that upholds the imamate.

After establishing the imamate, the author turns to the action that sustains it: enjoining good and forbidding evil. This passage declares it a communal obligation and explains why the poem only mentions commanding good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the link between imamate and this duty.
2
Identify the type of obligation
Find where the passage states 'وجوبًا كفائيًا' and understand what a communal obligation means.
This is a key concept in Islamic jurisprudence.
3
Understand the omission of 'forbidding evil'
The passage explains why the poet omitted forbidding evil: because commanding good implies it. Reflect on the wisdom of this choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر
١٣٤- وَأَمُرْ بِعُرْفٍ وَاجْتَنِبْ نَمِيمَهْ وَغِيبَةٌ وَخَصْلَةٌ ذَمِيمَا
ولما فرغ من الإمامة عقبها بما يتوقف القيام به غالبا عليها، وهو بالأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر، فقال: (وأمر بعرف) (وانه عن منكر) وجوبًا كفائيًا، وإنما ترك النهى عن المنكر لاستلزام الأمر له، وآثر الأمر لشرفه.
The obligation of enjoining good and forbidding evil. 134- 'And command what is good, and avoid backbiting, slander, and every blameworthy trait.' When he finished discussing the imamate, he followed it with that upon which its establishment often depends, namely enjoining good and forbidding evil. So he said: 'And command what is good' and 'forbid what is evil' as a communal obligation. He omitted the phrase 'forbidding evil' because commanding the good implies it, and he preferred 'command' because of its nobility.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the obligation of al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar. The author introduces it as a necessary complement to the imamate, since the imam's authority is typically maintained through this duty. He then cites the poem's verse which commands enjoining good and avoiding evil traits. The commentary explains that the obligation is communal (fard kifayah): if some fulfill it, the rest are absolved. The author notes that the poem only mentions commanding good and omits forbidding evil because commanding good necessarily implies forbidding its opposite. The reason for choosing 'command' is its honor, as it is associated with what is praiseworthy.

Key Terms

نهي عن المنكر
وجوب كفائي
فرغ من الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this duty is only for the ruler.
Correction: The passage states it is a communal obligation (فرد كفائي) upon all capable Muslims, not just the ruler.

Checkpoint

B) Communal obligation (fard kifayah).
C) Recommended act (mandub).
D) Permissible act (mubah).

Key Takeaway
Enjoining good and forbidding evil is a communal religious duty that every capable Muslim should participate in, as it supports the authority of the imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Relying on the Creed of Ahl al-Sunna
Concluding exhortation to rely on the Sunni creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the phrase 'fa'tamid' as a concluding exhortation to rely on the creed of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a as presented in the text.

Core Idea
The creed is complete; now rely on it with certainty.

This brief concluding passage marks the end of the section on creed. The author signals completion and urges the reader to embrace the presented doctrines with firm reliance, as they represent the orthodox Sunni position. The footnote clarifies a subtle point about the word 'tukmila' to prevent misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud, noting the phrase 'فَاعْتَمِدْ'. Observe how it functions as a conclusion.
The word 'تكملة' indicates completion.
2
Understand the translation
Read the literal translation. Notice that 'fa'tamid' is an imperative meaning 'so rely'.
3
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary explains that this line ends the creed section and directs the reader to rely on what has been taught because it is the Sunni position.
The footnote addresses a potential objection about the word 'تكملة'.
4
Take the checkpoint quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm your understanding of the passage's main point.
The answer is that it indicates completion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (فَاعْتَمِدْ) تكملةٌ ، أشارَ به إلى انقِضَاء فن العقائد وتمامه (۱) ، أي : فاعتمد في جزم العقيدة على ما ذكرتُهُ لكَ لأنَّه مذهب أهل السُّنَّة والجماعة .
(۱) قوله : (تكملة) أي: المقصود منه التكملة، والإشارة جاءت تبعا فلا يقال إذا كان تكملة كيف يكون فيه إشارة ؟ مع أن التكملة هي التي لا يقصد بها شيء. الشنواني.
And His statement: (so rely) is a completion. He indicated by it the conclusion of the art of creeds and its perfection (1). Meaning: rely in the certainty of the creed upon what I have mentioned to you, because it is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. (1) His statement: (completion) – i.e. its purpose is completion, and the indication came as a consequence; so it should not be said: if it is a completion, how is there an indication in it? While the completion is that which nothing is intended by it. By al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding remark to the section on creed (al-aqa'id). The author explains that the phrase 'fa'tamid' (so rely) is a completion (tukmila) of the discourse, signaling the end of the discussion on creed. It urges the reader to rely with certainty on what has been presented, because it is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. The footnote clarifies that the word 'tukmila' is used for completion and that the indication is incidental, not primary.
